Shanghai Shenbao Local Supplement Film Special

Saint of Love? Prodigal Son? Don Juan in the Eyes of Douglas Fairbanks 344

— Hua

Don Juan, everyone probably knows him, right? What kind of person is he after all? A hero? A rogue? Or is he a romantic master who hopes to get perfect love? Or a pornographic pervert who uses women for entertainment? Like this, it is really not easy to make a judgment if you don't have a deep understanding of Don Juan. He has so many concubines and beauties, and his romantic affairs are widely spread. What kind of person can you say he is?

Not long ago, Douglas Fairbanks left Hollywood to produce films in Europe. Under the leadership of the famous director Alexander Korda, he has completed a giant film about Don Juan's life of romance, "The Private Life of Don Juan" (which will be shown at the Nanjing Grand Theater in Shanghai). For this matter, he actually went to Seville, Spain—Don Juan's former residence—to study his life in detail. He understood Don Juan's life of distress, and knew that his love was not as crazy and romantic as outsiders imagined. He knew that Don Juan was an idealist. A big lover with oversensitive thoughts and rich emotions. He spent his life pursuing a perfect woman in his mind. Comparatively, he lived in Seville for the longest time, but this is not very important, just because Seville is his hometown. He spent his entire life pursuing his ideal perfect lover, but this pursuit always disappointed him. Because the woman he idealized was too good, too perfect, there was simply no such woman, and there never would be. What he pursued day and night was only what poets fantasized about, what musicians played, and what artists created, not a real person. Although the number of women he got in his life was countless, they were just ordinary people. Of course, they all had the beauty of sinking fish and falling geese, and the appearance of closing the moon and shaming flowers, but they were still far from his ideal.

Don Juan is handsome. What can be seen on the outside is his handsome appearance, and what is contained in his heart is rich passion. When women meet him, they will pursue him and refuse to leave him. For this reason, he had to wander around! In this way, he was said to be a "playboy"! Poor Don Juan! He spent his life's energy and never found the woman he admired. How can we say that he is a pornographic pervert? He avoided many women to escape, so how can we say that he is a playboy? The shortcoming of his life is because he cherished women too much. He could not bear to make them suffer the pain of disappointed love, so he accepted the criticism of "unfaithfulness." How can the world understand his inner hidden pain?

Although in our view, the woman he pursued was too idealized. However, when Don Juan was in Seville, the organization of life was not as mechanized as it is now. The place where he lived was in a poetic environment and romantic mood. Therefore, he had too much hope for the realization of his ideal. We really can't blame him.

The great English poet Byron also wrote a lot of descriptions of Don Juan's life, but the events written are not necessarily true facts. If we want to truly understand Don Juan, we have to actually collect all the biographies and anecdotes about him, analyze him, and then we can understand Don Juan's true face. Of course, a person's life has its length, its good and its evil, but the writer often likes to nitpick and write about his shortcomings, and magnify his evil deeds. His original true face is wiped out with one stroke.

In short, we must understand that Don Juan's life was to seek the perfect, flawless beauty in his ideal from the romantic process, but he was finally disappointed.

Film Review

Review of "One Exciting Adventure" 345

— Hua Lun

One Exciting

Adventure—Great Shanghai

This "One Exciting Adventure," which condescended to have Lande Chuan perform his skills as an accompanist, does not reduce its shortcomings due to its shortness in many mediocre detective films.

It concocts a group of inexplicable characters. The girl Lina goes everywhere to steal with her unparalleled beauty, saying that it is due to the playful attitude of neurasthenia. The young man Le Fo Sha sat in the car and followed her around. He paid the losses of the owners everywhere, saying it was because he loved her. Under this almost mysterious and bizarre setting, the plot unfolded. Detective Fox used the fragrance of the gloves she lost in a theft to pull out the perfumer Walder. Walder fell in love with her at first sight, and the three of them made a big fuss over their love affairs, plus that idiotic and silly Sin Sili's gag. As a result, it was discovered in the file that Le Fo Sha was originally a famous swindler. So he paid money to the two investigators to acquit her. At the same time, Lina recovered from her old illness and married the perfumer.

From the point of view of its performance, naturally, in such a script, there will be no special achievements. Like many mediocre detective films in the period of decline, the performance tries to avoid some boring exploration and serious investigation, and interweaves the progress of the case with love and song and dance. Even the detectives and other characters who handle the case are caricatured and gagged to increase the relaxed and interesting atmosphere. This is the way for general producers to hide their clumsiness, and it is also the inevitable manifestation of poor skills.

Director E. Franke is not seen here to be outstanding. He is only more successful in writing the mysterious and elusive parts of the heroine Lina, and trying to use the atmosphere to set off her personality.

Binnje Barnes' Lina is not mysterious and unrestrained enough when she is calm. She is also not unrestrained and fervent enough when she is passionate. The actors who play Walder and Le Fo Sha are even more mediocre. Grant Mitchell, who plays Fox, portrays a certain type of person in society quite incisively.

Review of "The Alluring History of Luo Fu" 346

— You Jing

Elinor Norton – A Cathay Production

The young girl Elinor abandons Harley, who loves her, and follows her grandmother's advice to marry Donald. Her husband's jealousy causes her great marital pain. After Donald goes off to war, she falls in love with another young man, Lynn. However, things don't always go as planned. Donald returns, and in addition to his jealous nature, the war has turned him into a neurotic patient. Elinor is now torn between pity for her husband and love for her lover, becoming a prisoner of love and jealousy. After much turmoil and conflict, they eventually reach the destination of love's union.

The original author, Mary Roberts Reinhart, already created a vulgar and superficial work. Rose Franken failed to treat it with cinematic artistry and simply created a lengthy and rambling film based on the original. In addition to jokes such as a jealous husband willing to partner in a ranch with his wife's lover and agreeing to a six-month trial period of love with his rival, the film emphasizes the coincidence of the husband falling off his horse in a remote area and the lover arriving by car to rescue him, using this as a key element of the entire drama. Unnecessary war scenes are inserted into the gentle tone of the play. Perhaps this was deliberately arranged to increase the play's power and to set off the scene of Elinor and her lover riding horses, but the abruptness and awkwardness are enough to disrupt the mood of the entire play, which is an obvious shortcoming.

Director Hamilton MacFadden has worked hard to achieve some success. Apart from being limited by the script, some individual scenes show considerable skill and cleverness. For example, the passionate love of the newlyweds before the jealousy erupts is written in a very mysterious and pure way. After the quarrel, the wife's psychological pain and her confession to her husband, as well as the snow scene, delicately entrust the mood of the characters to the surrounding things, which is commendable. The setting and handling of the climax are also more appropriate and sufficient, achieving considerable effect. After the sudden change at the climax, there is a refreshing ending. Similarly, feelings are entrusted to the scenery, showing the development of another realm. However, the depiction of the war has a hint of cutting corners, doesn't it? Since it was a fierce battle, the casualties would certainly not be like the scene on the screen, with only Donald and one or two others.

Among the actors, Claire Trevor is better as Elinor. Her portrayal of the conflicting emotions of love and pity is quite moving and represents progress compared to her role in "Little Miss Broadway." The role of Lynn was originally easy to succeed in, but Gilbert Roland did not achieve the deserved results. Hugh Williams seems to be very good at acting, but here he failed to adequately express the character's love for his wife and his jealous hatred. He was able to let the neurotic patient play out as well, but it passed by uneventfully.

Pola Negri Re-emerges as an Actress 347

— Dong Lin

Moviegoers with good memories will surely still remember Pola Negri, who enjoyed great fame in the silent film era. After disappearing from the screen for about three years, she has now returned to Hollywood as an actress once again.

Her first film is said to be based on the life of Frederic Chopin, the famous 19th-century Polish composer. Warner Brothers is currently negotiating various conditions with her, and because Pola likes the story very much, there probably won't be any problems.

But in fact, Pola doesn't seem to be paying much attention to returning to the screen. She is currently focusing on how to arrange the Valentino's house that she recently bought.

"I have decided to spend the rest of my life here," Pola told reporters, "and only in this house can I live happily, often reminiscing about the sweet love between Valentino and me. I think he must like me living here too!"

Did you know that Pola and Valentino were a couple in the past?

Chinese Master Technician Lang Deshan to Retire Soon 348

— Qin

Lang Deshan, a native of Shandong, has traveled the world for thirty years, promoting China's inherent technology abroad and gaining great renown. Three years ago, he returned to China once and performed at the Nanjing Grand Theater in this city. The success of ticket sales was unparalleled. Foreign residents in Europe and America have long admired Mr. Lang's technology and were eager to see it. And we Chinese people also hoped to see Mr. Lang's accomplishments in promoting national quintessence for the country. Mr. Lang's two daughters have reached the age of marriage, and he himself has reached retirement age. Three years ago, he intended to spend his remaining years in his homeland. However, due to the firm invitation of overseas theaters to perform for the last time, Mr. Lang reluctantly agreed. Over the past three years, he has traveled across Europe and America. Now he is returning home. His performance at the Grand Shanghai Theater in this city will probably also be his last time to meet the people of Shanghai. It is said that whether Mr. Lang will live in Shanghai for a long time is still undecided. After his two daughters are married off, he may return to his hometown to live out his days.

Movie Novel

Forsaking All Others (9) 349

— Su Yin

Dillon groaned a few more times, moaning, "Because I... Ooh... Ate something too bitter... Burned it... And... Bad luck came one after another. First, the car fell into the ditch. Then it rained, soaking me through. Then I burned myself. Now I've caught a cold. This day... This night..." Geoffrey looked at him strangely, then asked insincerely, "What about Marie? How is she?" Dillon pouted, "Marie! Why even ask about her. I think she's very comfortable there. She left me and left. She wasn't burned... She didn't even catch a cold. I'm afraid she slept more comfortably last night than ever before... I don't even know where she slept."

Geoffrey listened, at first not daring to believe his own ears. After a while, she suddenly burst out laughing. He laughed and laughed, laughing uncontrollably. Dillon asked, "What's wrong? So happy?" Geoffrey shook his head, "You wouldn't understand. Get up, Casanova." He then helped Dillon up, helped him get dressed, and prepared to go home.

On their way home, they could have been safe and sound. But halfway, they met Connie again. Connie and Johnson were looking for her husband together. But on the way, the tire burst. Connie got out of the car and paced back and forth on the road, fuming. Johnson crawled on the ground to fix the tire. Geoffrey's car arrived at this place and quickly slammed on the brakes. Connie glared at Dillon fiercely. At this time, Dillon was sitting next to the driver in the front, indescribably miserable. He looked at Connie with wide, unfocused eyes and a pleading expression, saying, "Connie... Let's talk it over..." Connie heard this and roared like thunder, "Me? Talk it over? You bastard! Look at you, what a wonderful family reunion!" He shouted and argued, even Paula, Marie, and Geoffrey, who were sitting in the back, were scolded along with him. "What exactly is going on? Tell me. But even if you don't tell me, can't I guess?"

Geoffrey, of course, had to step forward again. He politely said to Mrs. Duda, "It's not good to talk on the road now. If you're willing to get in our car, we'll naturally come up with a solution when we get to the city." Connie reluctantly listened to him and got into their car. Geoffrey started negotiating with Connie along the way. Marie was constantly mocking and scolding Geoffrey from time to time. But Geoffrey ignored her. He soon negotiated with Connie. He asked Dillon to give her one million dollars in alimony, and they would divorce. Dillon listened in the front and had no choice but to vaguely agree. Marie, of course, had nothing to say.

Soon, they had arrived in front of Dillon's residence. All the problems had been solved. Dillon had no choice but to go inside, take his belongings, and move to the club or go to the hospital to recover. Connie, on the other hand, scheduled to go to Mexico tomorrow to complete the divorce procedures. Geoffrey helped Dillon out of the car, up the steps, and into the elevator. But when he backed out and walked outside the gate, Marie and Paula had already driven away in the original car and were already out of reach.

Geoffrey couldn't help but curse inwardly and quickly hailed a streetcar to chase after them. By the time he caught up with Marie, Marie was already about to enter her house. Geoffrey jumped out of the car, rushed forward, and grabbed Marie's arm, saying, "Marie... Are you angry with me?" Marie struggled, trying to push his hand away, but couldn't break free. She shouted, "Let go... My arm hurts..." Geoffrey begged, "That was unavoidable. Connie was prepared to make a big deal out of this. I had no choice but to stabilize her first... Let her think you were willing to marry Dillon." Marie suddenly turned around and said to him, "Let her think I was willing to marry Dillon? I'm definitely going to marry him. What do you think?"
